Prince George's County v. Ray's Used Cars


No. 133, September Term, 2005


COURT OF APPEALS OF MARYLAND


398 Md. 632; 922 A.2d 495; 2007 Md. LEXIS 259



May 4, 2007, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Corrected May 7, 2007. As Corrected May 8, 2007.


PRIOR HISTORY: Certiorari to the Court of Special Appeals (Circuit Court for Prince George's County). Michele D. Hotten, JUDGE.
Prince George's County v. Ray's Used Cars, 391 Md. 577, 894 A.2d 545, 2006 Md. LEXIS 963 (2006)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Court of Special Appeals (Maryland) entered a judgment that affirmed a trial court's judgment that a county zoning ordinance establishing a minimum lot size of 25,000 square feet for used vehicle lots violated substantive due process and equal protection principles, and which enjoined its enforcement. Petitioner county filed a petition for writ of certiorari, which the state's highest court granted.


OVERVIEW: The county enacted a zoning ordinance that imposed a minimum lot size requirement of 25,000 square feet in used vehicle dealerships. Respondent used car dealers filed an action in the trial court to challenge the constitutionality of the ordinance, as well as to seek declaratory and injunctive relief. The used car dealers all had standing to challenge the ordinance because they all operated used car lots that did not meet the new minimum lot size requirement of 25,000 square feet. However, the used car dealers did not institute any administrative proceedings seeking variances or other administrative remedies. The trial held a nonjury trial to consider the impact the ordinance would have on their particular businesses. The trial court then ruled that the ordinance violated substantive due process and equal protection principles. It also enjoined enforcement of the ordinance. The intermediate appellate court then affirmed the trial court's ruling. On further review, the state's highest court found that the used car dealers were required to invoke and exhaust their administrative remedies and they did not show that any exception to the exhaustion requirement applied.


OUTCOME: The intermediate appellate court's judgment was reversed and the case was remanded to the intermediate appellate court with directions to reverse the trial court's judgment and remand the case to the trial court with instructions to dismiss the action.

 

174 of 744 DOCUMENTS


McNack v. State


No. 98, September Term, 2006


COURT OF APPEALS OF MARYLAND


398 Md. 378; 920 A.2d 1097; 2007 Md. LEXIS 178



April 12, 2007, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Circuit Court for Baltimore City pursuant to certiorari to the Court of Special Appeals. M. Brooke Murdock, JUDGE.
McNack v. State, 396 Md. 11, 912 A.2d 647, 2006 Md. LEXIS 889 (2006)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant relatives of decedents sued appellees, state and local government entities and officials, alleging that appellees violated the decedents state constitutional rights and negligently failed to protect the decedents who were killed when their home was fire-bombed by drug dealers. The relatives appealed the judgment of the Circuit Court for Baltimore City (Maryland) which dismissed the relatives' complaint.


OVERVIEW: The relatives contended that appellees actively sought the cooperation of the public in combatting illicit drug trade, but failed to protect the decedents from retaliation after the decedents reported drug deals. The relatives argued that appellees violated the decedents' state constitutional rights by failing to protect the decedents from a danger created by appellees, and that appellees were negligent in their efforts to protect the decedents. The appellate court held that the relatives failed to state any claim against appellees. The State of Maryland did not recognize the state-created-danger theory as a basis upon which to recover for violations of Maryland's Constitution and, in any event, there was no affirmative act of appellees toward the decedents to implicate the theory. Further, no special relationship between appellees and the decedents was created by the decedents' reports, appellees' responses to the reports, or appellees promise to place the decedents on a special protection list. Thus, in the absence of a special relationship, appellees general duty to protect the public was not extended to a special duty to protect the decedents.


OUTCOME: The judgment dismissing the relatives' complaint was affirmed.

Sapero v. Mayor of Baltimore


No. 72, September Term, 2006


COURT OF APPEALS OF MARYLAND


398 Md. 317; 920 A.2d 1061; 2007 Md. LEXIS 179



April 12, 2007, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Circuit Court for Baltimore City (Quick-Take Condemnation Case). Stuart R. Berger, JUDGE,



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT OF THE CIRCUIT COURT FOR BALTIMORE CITY VACATED. CASE REMANDED TO THAT COURT FOR FURTHER PROCEEDINGS CONSISTENT WITH THIS OPINION. COSTS TO BE PAID BY APPELLEE.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Baltimore City Circuit Court (Maryland) entered a judgment that granted appellee city's petitions for immediate possession of appellant property owner's properties via a procedure known as a "quick-take," pursuant to Baltimore, Md., Code of Public Local Laws § 21-16. The property owner appealed.


OVERVIEW: The city began work on an urban renewal plan for a certain area of the city. To that end, the city sought to condemn several private properties via a process known as a "quick-take" condemnation, which was authorized under certain circumstances pursuant to Baltimore, Md., Code of Public Local Laws § 21-16. The properties were owned by the property owner. An affidavit attached to the city's petitions to take the properties stated that the properties had to be in possession of the city at the earliest possible time in order to assist in a business expansion of the area. The trial court held a "quick-take" hearing. At no point did the city show that any emergency existed that made it necessary for the city to obtain immediate possession of the properties. The trial court then granted the city's petitions for immediate possession of the property owner's properties. On appeal, the state's highest court found that the city had the burden of showing why, because of some emergency, it was in the public interest for the city to take immediate possession of the properties and the city made no such showing.


OUTCOME: The trial court's judgment was vacated and the case was remanded to the trial court for further proceedings.

Forster v. Hargadon


Misc. No. 8, September Term, 2006


COURT OF APPEALS OF MARYLAND

CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner public defender filed a petition seeking a writ of prohibition, writ of mandamus, or other appropriate relief to vacate a directive involving the filing of exceptions in juvenile cases issued by respondent trial court that the public defender believed were both substantively unlawful and constituted an impermissible local rule. The state's highest court granted the public defender's motion to stay pending a decision on the petition.

The public defender believed the directive was both substantively unlawful and constituted an impermissible local rule. It imposed certain procedural requirements on parties who filed exceptions to the report of a master in juvenile cases and permitted the court to dismiss the exceptions if those requirements were not met. The directive commanded the court clerk to enter the order in each exceptions case, so that the order and its requirements were case-specific. The state's highest court denied the public defender's petition for relief and revoked a stay it had entered while it was considering the petition request. It determined that it should not grant such relief where a requesting party had a specific and adequate legal remedy to meet the justice of a particular case. It then noted that the very issues the public defender was concerned about were currently pending in two cases in the state's intermediate appellate court.


OUTCOME: The state's highest court dismissed the petition and revoked the stay it had granted.

 

177 of 744 DOCUMENTS


Abrams v. Lamone


No. 142, September Term, 2005


COURT OF APPEALS OF MARYLAND


398 Md. 146; 919 A.2d 1223; 2007 Md. LEXIS 126



March 26, 2007, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Amended March 29, 2007. As Amended May 21, 2007.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Circuit Court for Anne Arundel County pursuant to certiorari to the Court of Special Appeals. Paul A. Hackner, JUDGE.
Abrams v. Lamone, 394 Md. 304, 905 A.2d 840, 2006 Md. LEXIS 539 (2006)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant voter filed an action in the Circuit Court for Anne Arundel County (Maryland) against appellee candidate, State Administrator of Elections, and State Board, seeking declaratory and injunctive relief on the grounds that the candidate did not have the qualifications for the office of Attorney General of Maryland. The candidate was granted summary judgment. The voter appealed and filed a petition for writ of certiorari, which was granted.


OVERVIEW: The issue was whether the candidate met the requirements of Md. Const. art. V, § 4. Under the constitutional provision's plain language, the appellate court read "practiced law in this State for at least ten years" to mean that one who sought to be Attorney General must have practiced law for 10 years, in Maryland, as a member of the Maryland Bar. In essence, the practice requirement related not solely to the practice of law in Maryland, but to its practice in Maryland as a member of the Maryland Bar. The appellate court's interpretation was consistent with the history of the constitutional provision and its context. The candidate's federal bar membership, which allowed him to practice law in Maryland, did not count toward his eligibility to be the Attorney General as neither 28 U.S.C.S. § 517 nor U.S. Dist. Ct., D. Md., R. 701(1)(b), authorized a Maryland state law practice. Thus, the circuit court erred in holding that the candidate had practiced law in Maryland for 10 years. The candidate's admission to the Maryland Bar six years ago, coupled with his lengthy, but primarily, federal practice of law was insufficient to meet the requirements of Md. Const. art. V, § 4.


OUTCOME: The circuit court's ruling was reversed. The candidate was ineligible to be a candidate for Attorney General.

Thornton v. State


No. 62, September Term, 2005


COURT OF APPEALS OF MARYLAND


397 Md. 704; 919 A.2d 678; 2007 Md. LEXIS 118



March 20, 2007, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Certiorari to the Court of Special Appeals (Circuit Court for Baltimore County) J. Norris Byrnes, JUDGE.
Thornton v. State, 162 Md. App. 719, 876 A.2d 142, 2005 Md. App. LEXIS 73 (2005)



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT OF THE COURT OF SPECIAL APPEALS REVERSED. THE CASE IS REMANDED TO THAT COURT WITH DIRECTIONS TO REVERSE THE JUDGMENT OF THE CIRCUIT COURT FOR BALTIMORE COUNTY AND REMAND THE CASE TO THAT COURT FOR THE PURPOSE OF A NEW TRIAL. BALTIMORE COUNTY TO PAY THE COSTS IN THIS COURT AND IN THE COURT OF SPECIAL APPEALS.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner inmate's petition for a writ of certiorari was granted with regard to petitioner challenging the judgment of the Court of Special Appeals (Maryland), which affirmed petitioner's conviction for murder in the second degree. Petitioner was originally charged with first degree murder but was found guilty of murder in the second degree instead.


OVERVIEW: Defendant's conviction arose from the stabbing death of another youth at a shopping mall. The court held that the special appellate court erred in affirming the trial court's interpretation and application of the intent element for the crime of second-degree murder. The court determined that, as trier of fact, the trial judge was permitted, but not required, to infer from petitioner's wilful act of thrusting the knife outward and into the victim that petitioner intended to commit such grievous bodily harm from which death would likely ensue; however, the trier of fact was not permitted to presume, from petitioner's conduct, that he intended to inflict grievous bodily harm as a matter of law or to presume anything from his use of the knife. The trial judge's mistaken conclusions of law, which modified the specific intent requirement and unconstitutionally shifted the burden of proof to petitioner, warranted reversal of petitioner's conviction for murder in the second degree and entitled him to a new trial.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the special appellate court and remanded the case to that court with directions for it to reverse the judgment of the trial court and remand the case to the trial court for a new trial.

Dep't of Health & Mental Hygiene v. Kelly


No. 47, September Term, 2006


COURT OF APPEALS OF MARYLAND


397 Md. 399; 918 A.2d 470; 2007 Md. LEXIS 102



March 14, 2007, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Circuit Court for Baltimore City pursuant to certiorari to Court of Special Appeals. Albert J. Matricciani, Jr., JUDGE.



DISPOSITION:  [***1] JUDGMENT OF THE CIRCUIT COURT OF BALTIMORE CITY AFFIRMED. COSTS TO BE PAID BY APPELLANT.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Montgomery County Circuit Court (Maryland) entered a judgment that reversed a decision of the administrative law judge upholding appellant state mental health department's determination that appellee civil detainee should be forcibly medicated. After the state mental health department appealed to the intermediate appellate court, the state's highest court on its own issued a writ of certiorari to review the case.


OVERVIEW: The civil detainee was adjudged incompetent to stand trial in the trial court on multiple criminal charges filed against him because his delusional disorder prevented him from understanding the adversarial nature of the proceedings, and precluded him from assisting in his criminal defense. He was committed to a state health institution for treatment. The state mental health department convened a Clinical Review Panel, which approved his forcible medication of the civil detainee; that decision was upheld by an administrative law judge. The trial court reversed and the state's highest court, which on its own issued a writ of certiorari to review the case, held that Md. Code Ann., Health-Gen. § 10-708(g) (Repl. Vol. 2005) required the State to prove that an individual, because of his mental illness, was dangerous to himself or others within a state institution before it could forcibly administer medication. The state's highest court determined that there was nothing in the record indicating that he was, because of his mental illness, dangerous to himself or others within the state institution where he was being held. Thus, it found that he could not be forcibly medicated.


OUTCOME: The trial court's judgment was affirmed.

Cathcart v. State


No. 64, September Term, 2006


COURT OF APPEALS OF MARYLAND


397 Md. 320; 916 A.2d 1008; 2007 Md. LEXIS 72



February 9, 2007, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Writ of habeas corpus dismissed, Certificate of appealability denied Cathcart v. Maryland, 2013 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 140574 (D. Md., Sept. 30, 2013)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Certiorari to the Court of Special Appeals (Circuit Court for Prince George's County). Graydon S. McKee, III, JUDGE. In the Circuit Court for Prince George's County. Case No. CT040584X.
Cathcart v. State, 169 Md. App. 379, 901 A.2d 262, 2006 Md. App. LEXIS 93 (2006)



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT OF COURT OF SPECIAL APPEALS VACATED; CASE REMANDED TO THAT COURT WITH INSTRUCTIONS TO REMAND CASE TO CIRCUIT COURT FOR PRINCE GEORGE'S COUNTY WITH INSTRUCTIONS TO AMEND SENTENCE IMPOSED ON FALSE IMPRISONMENT CONVICTION TO IMPRISONMENT FOR TEN YEARS, CONSECUTIVE TO SENTENCE IMPOSED ON FIRST DEGREE ASSAULT CONVICTION; COSTS IN THIS COURT AND IN COURT OF SPECIAL APPEALS TO BE PAID BY PRINCE GEORGE'S COUNTY.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted in the trial court of first degree assault and false imprisonment. For the assault, he was sentenced to ten years, and, for the false imprisonment, he was sentenced to consecutive life imprisonment with all but ten years suspended. No probation was imposed as to the suspended sentence. The Court of Special Appeals (Maryland) affirmed the sentence, and defendant sought further review.


OVERVIEW: Defendant said his life sentence was unconstitutionally disproportionate. The court of appeals held defendant's sentences were not illegal. The sentence imposed on the assault conviction was well within the permissible statutory range, and the sentence imposed for false imprisonment, despite its wording, was not a life sentence and had no attribute or collateral consequence of a life sentence. The court effectively imposed two ten-year sentences, one consecutive to the other, which were not unlawful. If, under the sentence as articulated and presumably intended, defendant could never serve over ten years for false imprisonment, the sentence imposed on that conviction could not reasonably be regarded as a life sentence. Because the effective false imprisonment sentence was ten years, it had no attributes of a life sentence, for parole eligibility or any other purpose. As a housekeeping measure, the judgment as recorded had to be amended to reflect the true nature of the sentence.


OUTCOME: The lower court's judgment was vacated, and the matter was remanded to the trial court to amend its judgment to reflect the true nature of defendant's false imprisonment sentence.

 

182 of 744 DOCUMENTS



Mayor of Baltimore City v. Valsamaki


No. 55, September Term, 2006


COURT OF APPEALS OF MARYLAND


397 Md. 222; 916 A.2d 324; 2007 Md. LEXIS 69



February 8, 2007, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Circuit Court for Baltimore City (Quick-Take Condemnation Case). John Philip Miller JUDGE. Circuit Court for Baltimore City. Case # 298006008-9.



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT OF THE CIRCUIT COURT FOR BALTIMORE CITY AFFIRMED. COSTS TO BE PAID BY THE APPELLANT.



CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, a city, filed a quick-take condemnation action against property pursuant to Code of Public Local Laws of Baltimore City, Md., § 21-16(a). Appellee, the owner of the property, opposed the action. The Circuit Court for Baltimore City (Maryland) issued a memorandum opinion denying the city's petitions. After a motion to reconsider had been denied, the city noted a direct appeal.


OVERVIEW: The property in question consisted of a three story building which housed a bar and package goods store. The appellate court held that under the Code of Public Local Laws of Baltimore City, Md., § 21-16(a), the city was required to demonstrate the reason or reasons why it was necessary for it to have immediate possession and immediate title to a particular property via the exercise of a quick-take condemnation. The appellate court held that the city failed to present specific evidence to meet this requirement. The petition simply stated that the property would be used for redevelopment purposes and to assist in a business expansion of the area. This statement, in and of itself, while perhaps sufficient to justify regular condemnation, did not justify a quick-take condemnation. The appellate court held that the only plan for the property was that a private developer would possibly, at some future time, create a plan that the city might approve. The appellate court held that this was not sufficient to demonstrate an immediate public interest necessitating the city's use of quick-take condemnation.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.


 

183 of 744 DOCUMENTS


Koshko v. Haining


No. 35, September Term, 2006


COURT OF APPEALS OF MARYLAND


398 Md. 404; 921 A.2d 171; 2007 Md. LEXIS 10



January 12, 2007, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Corrected March 9, 2007.


PRIOR HISTORY: Certiorari to the Court of Special Appeals (Curcuit Court for Baltimore County. Ruth Ann Jakubowski, Judge. Case # 03-C-04-4273.
Koshko v. Haining, 168 Md. App. 556, 897 A.2d 866, 2006 Md. App. LEXIS 64 (2006)



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT OF THE COURT OF SPECIAL APPEALS REVERSED; CASE REMANDED TO THAT COURT WITH DIRECTIONS TO REVERSE THE JUDGMENT OF THE CIRCUIT COURT FOR BALTIMORE COUNTY AND TO REMAND THE CASE TO THE CIRCUIT COURT FOR FURTHER PROCEEDINGS CONSISTENT WITH THIS OPINION; COSTS IN THIS COURT AND IN THE COURT OF SPECIAL APPEALS TO BE DIVIDED EQUALLY BETWEEN THE PARTIES.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Court of Special Appeals, Maryland, affirmed the circuit court judgment rejecting petitioner parents' challenge to the constitutionality of Maryland's grandparental visitation statute (GVS), Md. Code Ann., Fam. Law § 9-102 (1984, 2004 repl. vol.), and granting respondent grandparents' petition for visitation of their minor grandchildren. The parents filed a petition for writ of certiorari, which was granted.


OVERVIEW: The parents challenged the constitutionality of the GVS on its face and as applied to them, based on their fundamental right, safeguarded by the Due Process Clause of U.S. Const. amend. XIV, to direct and control their children's upbringing. The court rejected the facial challenge by reading into the GVS a presumption, recognized under the Due Process Clause and Maryland common law, that parental decisions regarding their children were valid. So construed, the GVS was facially valid. However, the GVS was unconstitutional as applied because it potentially worked a direct and substantial interference with the parents' fundamental right to parent. While the State had a compelling interest in encouraging the grandparents' contributions to their grandchildren's lives, the GVS required narrow tailoring to vindicate the State's interest without burdening the parents' fundamental right. Thus, the GVS required a finding of parental unfitness or exceptional circumstances showing detriment to the children, absent visitation from the grandparents, as a prerequisite to application of the best interests analysis. Absent such a finding, the GVS was unconstitutional as applied to the parents.


OUTCOME: The special court of appeals' judgment was reversed, and the case was remanded to that court with directions to reverse the circuit court's judgment and to remand the case to the circuit court for further proceedings.

Aventis Pasteur, Inc. v. Skevofilax


No. 15, September Term, 2006


COURT OF APPEALS OF MARYLAND


396 Md. 405; 914 A.2d 113; 2007 Md. LEXIS 8



January 8, 2007, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Certoprari to the Court of Special Appeals (Circuit Court for Baltimore City). Stuart R. Berger, JUDGE. Case # 24-C-03-002575.
Skevofilax v. Aventis Pasteur, Inc., 167 Md. App. 1, 891 A.2d 416, 2006 Md. App. LEXIS 13 (2006)



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT OF THE COURT OF SPECIAL APPEALS REVERSED; CASE REMANDED TO THAT COURT WITH INSTRUCTIONS TO AFFIRM THE JUDGMENT OF THE CIRCUIT COURT FOR BALTIMORE CITY; COSTS IN THIS COURT AND THE COURT OF SPECIAL APPEALS TO BE PAID BY RESPONDENTS.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff-respondents, individually and as next friends of their son, filed suit seeking damages from defendant-petitioners, corporations engaged in the manufacture of pediatric vaccines and ingredients. The Court of Special Appeals (Maryland) reversed the denial of respondents' motion for dismissal of the complaint without prejudice and grant of summary judgment in the corporations' favor.


OVERVIEW: Respondents claimed in their complaint that their son's autism spectrum disorder was caused by thimerosal used in pediatric vaccines administered to their son as an infant. It was neither an abuse of discretion by the trial court to deny respondents' motion to dismiss nor error of law to enter summary judgment in favor of the corporations. The trial judge found that significant time and money had been expended by both sides in this litigation. The trial judge found that there had been no lack of diligence on the part of respondents' local Maryland counsel in representing them. Next, that a party provided an adequate explanation for the need to dismiss without prejudice did not compel the trial judge to grant the motion. Moreover, if motions for summary judgment were not pending formally at the time of the Md. R. 2-506(b) motion, respondents hardly could have been uncognizant of the likelihood that renewed motions for summary judgment would be forthcoming, following their expert's withdrawal, considering especially that the corporations filed dispositive motions at virtually each earlier critical juncture of the litigation.


OUTCOME: The instant court reversed the judgment of the court of special appeals.

Evans v. State


Nos. 107, 123, 124, September Term, 2005, No. 122, September Term, 2005


COURT OF APPEALS OF MARYLAND


396 Md. 256; 914 A.2d 25; 2006 Md. LEXIS 836



December 19, 2006, Filed

PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Circuit Court for Balitmore County pursuant to mtoin to correct Illegal Sentence-Dealth Penalty. Christian M. Kahl, JUDGE.
Evans v. State, 389 Md. 456, 886 A.2d 562, 2005 Md. LEXIS 649 (2005)



DISPOSITION:  [***1] IN NOS. 107, 123, AND 124, JUDGMENT OF CIRCUIT COURT FOR BALTIMORE COUNTY AFFIRMED, WITH COSTS; IN NO. 122, ORDER OF CIRCUIT COURT FOR BALTIMORE CITY DENYING TEMPORARY RESTRAINING ORDER VACATED; CASE REMANDED TO THAT COURT WITH INSTRUCTIONS TO ENJOIN ENFORCEMENT OF LETHAL INJECTION CHECKLIST INCLUDED AS PART OF DIVISION OF CORRECTION EXECUTION OPERATIONS MANUAL UNTIL SUCH TIME AS THE CONTENTS OF THAT CHECKLIST, IN THEIR CURRENT OR ANY AMENDED FORM, ARE ADOPTED AS REGULATIONS IN ACCORDANCE WITH THE REQUIREMENTS OF THE ADMINISTRATIVE PROCEDURE ACT OR THE GENERAL ASSEMBLY EXEMPTS THE CHECKLIST FROM THE REQUIREMENTS OF THAT ACT; COSTS IN NO. 122 TO BE PAID BY APPELLEE.

PROCEDURAL POSTURE: After the case was removed, a jury in the Circuit Court for Worcester County (Maryland) convicted defendant of two counts of first degree murder and sentenced him to death. After the case was removed and returned to the Circuit Court for Baltimore County (Maryland), a jury sentenced defendant to death at a resentencing hearing. Defendant appealed the denial of his postconviction motions and his motion for a temporary restraining order.


OVERVIEW: Defendant murdered the victim and a bystander to prevent the victim and his wife from testifying in a trial. On appeal, the court found that defendant's complaints regarding a new sentencing hearing based on ineffective assistance of counsel for counsel having failed to investigate and present mitigating evidence and a new trial based on the prosecution exercising peremptory strikes in a racially discriminatory manner were not cognizable in a motion under Md. Servs. § 3-905, which prescribed the method of execution by lethal injection, they constituted a regulation under Md. Code Ann., State Gov't § 10-101(g) that was not adopted in conformance with the procedural requirements of the Maryland Administrative Procedure Act.


OUTCOME: The judgments of the sentencing court as to the denials of defendant's postconviction motions were affirmed. The order of the sentencing court denying the temporary restraining order was vacated, and the case was remanded with instructions to enjoin the enforcement of the lethal injection checklist in the Execution Operations Manual until the list was properly adopted as regulations or exempted by the Maryland General Assembly.

Roskelly v. Lamone


No. 141, September Term, 2005


COURT OF APPEALS OF MARYLAND


396 Md. 27; 912 A.2d 658; 2006 Md. LEXIS 827



December 11, 2006, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Related proceeding at Lamone v. Capozzi, 396 Md. 53, 912 A.2d 674, 2006 Md. LEXIS 956 (Md., Dec. 11, 2006)


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Circuit Court for Anne Arundel County pursuant to certiorari to the Court of Special Appeals. Paul A. Hackner, JUDGE.
Roskelly v. Lamone, 393 Md. 363, 902 A.2d 1173, 2006 Md. LEXIS 458 (2006)



DISPOSITION:  [***1] COSTS TO BE PAID BY THE APPELLANTS.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant referendum petition proponent sought judicial review, in the Circuit Court for Anne Arundel County (Maryland), of appellee secretary of state's finding that his referendum petition had insufficient signatures. The trial court found the proponent's petition for judicial review was time barred, and the proponent appealed to the Court of Appeals and filed a petition for writ of certiorari, which the Court of Appeals granted.


OVERVIEW: When the proponent filed the signatures he had obtained by June 1 with the secretary of state, he was told he had not obtained valid signatures of at least one percent of the voters and was not, therefore, entitled to an additional 30 days to obtain the signatures of the other two percent of the voters required to refer the subject of his petition to a popular vote. He did not seek judicial review of this determination within ten days. The court of appeals held Md. Const. art. XVI, § 3(b) allowed him to file the signatures required to submit the subject of his petition to a popular vote in two steps, consisting of filing valid signatures of at least one percent of the voters by June 1, triggering an additional 30-day period within which the signatures of the additional two percent of the voters could be gathered. The signatures the proponent filed by June 1 were a petition, and, when the secretary of state found the number of those signatures to be insufficient to trigger the additional 30-day period, the proponent had 10 days to seek judicial review, under Md. Code Ann., Elec. Law § 6-210. When he did not seek review within this time, such review was time barred.


OUTCOME: The trial court's judgment was affirmed.

The trial court granted petitioner a new trial while petitioner's appeal from the judgment and sentence was pending in the intermediate appellate court. Both the State's and petitioner's cross-petitions for certiorari were granted.


OVERVIEW: The intermediate appellate court filed its written opinion affirming the trial court's initial judgment and sentence. The court also noted that the State failed to present any reason why the trial court did not have the authority to grant petitioner a new trial.


OUTCOME: The court vacated the judgment of the intermediate appellate court and remanded the case to that court with directions to dismiss the appeal on the grounds of mootness.

Purich v. Draper Props.


No. 9, September Term, 2006


COURT OF APPEALS OF MARYLAND


395 Md. 694; 912 A.2d 598; 2006 Md. LEXIS 821



December 7, 2006, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Circuit Court for Montgomery County pursuant to certiorari to the Court of Special Appeals. William J. Rowan, III, JUDGE.
Purich v. Draper, 393 Md. 160, 900 A.2d 206, 2006 Md. LEXIS 689 (2006)



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT OF THE CIRCUIT COURT FOR MONTGOMERY COUNTY VACATED. CASE REMANDED TO THAT COURT WITH DIRECTIONS TO REMAND THE CASE TO THE BOARD OF APPEALS FOR FURTHER PROCEEDINGS CONSISTENT WITH THIS OPINION. COSTS TO BE PAID BY THE APPELLEES.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The court granted certiorari, allowing appellants, two adjacent property owners, to challenge the judgment of the Circuit Court for Montgomery County (Maryland), which upheld the revocation of a special exception and the grant of a nonconforming use by appellee, a county zoning board of appeals, to appellee property owner, for the property owner to operate an automobile filling station.


OVERVIEW: The automobile filling station was operated on the property under the auspices of a special exception granted to the property owner, even though none of the improvements attached as conditions to the special exception by the board were made. Pursuant to the request of the property owner's new lessee, the board revoked the special exception and found that the lawful nonconforming use remained. The court found that the property at issue was in operation as an automobile filling station pursuant to a special exception. Once that use began, either the nonconforming use was immediately terminated or, at a minimum, the six month period of abandonment started in respect to the prior nonconforming use status of the property. Those six months passed and the nonconforming use, if not sooner terminated, was abandoned. As a result, the case required remand for the board to redetermine the status of the special exception.


OUTCOME: The court vacated the judgment and remanded the case to the trial court with directions for it to remand the case to the board for further proceedings.

Jeandell v. State


No. 113, September Term, 2005


COURT OF APPEALS OF MARYLAND


395 Md. 556; 910 A.2d 1141; 2006 Md. LEXIS 755



November 15, 2006, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Certiorari to the Court of Special Appeals (Circuit Court for Montgomery County). Case No. 99987. Eric M. Johnson, JUDGE.
Jeandell v. State, 165 Md. App. 26, 884 A.2d 739, 2005 Md. App. LEXIS 255 (2005)



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT OF THE COURT OF SPECIAL APPEALS REVERSED. CASE REMANDED TO THAT COURT WITH INSTRUCTIONS TO REVERSE THE JUDGMENT OF THE CIRCUIT COURT FOR MONTGOMERY COUNTY. COSTS IN THIS COURT AND THE COURT OF SPECIAL APPEALS TO BE PAID BY MONTGOMERY COUNTY.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted in the circuit court of violating Md. Code Ann., Crim. Proc. § 11-721(a) by knowingly failing to notify the State of his change in residences as required by Md. Code Ann., Crim. Proc. § 11-705(d). The Court of Special Appeals (Maryland) affirmed. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant's counsel argued that defendant could not comply with Md. Code Ann., Crim. Proc. § 11-721(a) (2005 Cum. Supp.) as written because defendant was homeless. The court of appeals held that the court of special appeals erred in finding that the evidence was sufficient to support a conviction beyond a reasonable doubt because the court of special appeals applied an incorrect interpretation of "residence" as used in Md. Code Ann., Crim. Proc. § 11-705(d). The court of appeals noted that if "residence" were simply a "living location," as the court of special appeals found, a homeless registrant might have to notify the Department of Public Safety and Correctional Service of a change in residences at least every seven days, if not more frequently, with the prospect that the new residence listed in each notice may be out of date and inaccurate. The court of appeals concluded that such a result was inconsistent with the framework of the statute. Because the trial court clearly concluded that defendant was homeless and did not have a place to go, it followed that no rational trier of fact could have concluded that defendant had a "residence" within the meaning of § 11-705(d).


OUTCOME: The judgment of the court of special appeals was reversed. The case was remanded with instructions to reverse the judgment of the circuit court.

Twine v. State


No. 138, September Term, 2005


COURT OF APPEALS OF MARYLAND


395 Md. 539; 910 A.2d 1132; 2006 Md. LEXIS 757



November 15, 2006, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Circuit Court for Montgomery County pursuant to certiorari to the Court of Special Appeals. Case No. 101708. Paul H. Weinstein, JUDGE.
Twine v. State, 392 Md. 724, 898 A.2d 1004, 2006 Md. LEXIS 967 (2006)



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT OF THE CIRCUIT COURT FOR MONTGOMERY COUNTY REVERSED. COSTS TO BE PAID BY MONTGOMERY COUNTY.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Circuit Court of Montgomery County (Maryland) convicted defendant of failing to register as a sexually violent offender by failing to provide notice of change of address to the Department of Public Safety and Correctional Services in violation of Md. Code Ann., Crim. Proc. § 11-721 (2005 Cum. Supp.). Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued that the evidence at trial was insufficient to support his conviction, as he could not register a change of residence because he had no residence to register. The State contended that defendant's residence changed when he became homeless and that defendant could have reported this change by notifying the appropriate authority of the location or locations he was occupying once he became homeless. The court of appeals held that defendant did not need to change his address within the meaning of Md. Code Ann., Crim. Proc. § 11-705(d) (2005 Cum. Supp.) when became homeless because defendant did not acquire a new "residence" within the meaning of § 11-705(d). The court of appeals concluded that a registrant has a "residence" within the meaning of § 11-705(d) only if that person has a fixed location at which the registrant is living or one to which the registrant intended to return upon leaving. Given that defendant was staying wherever he could, no rational trier of fact could have found that defendant had a fixed location to which he intended to return. Accordingly, no rational trier of fact could have concluded that defendant moved to a new "residence."


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court was reversed.

Neifert v. Dep't of the Env't


No. 10, September Term, 2006


COURT OF APPEALS OF MARYLAND


395 Md. 486; 910 A.2d 1100; 2006 Md. LEXIS 754; 64 ERC (BNA) 1685



November 14, 2006, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Circuit Court for Worcester County pursuant to certiorari to the Court of Special Appeals. Theodore R. Eschenburg, JUDGE.



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT OF THE CIRCUIT COURT FOR WORCESTER COUNTY AFFIRMED. COSTS TO BE PAID BY APPELLANTS.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant lot owners challenged a judgment of the Circuit Court for Worcester County (Maryland), which upheld appellee state environmental department's (department) decision to deny the owners sewer service and wetland fill permits. The trial court granted the department's motion for summary judgment, ruling that the denial of wetland fill permits and sewer hookups did not constitute a taking or violate the owners' equal protection rights.


OVERVIEW: Responding to contaminated drinking water, a county required lots to pass a seasonal percolation test, and the owners' lots were denied on-site septic system permits. The Environmental Protection Agency provided a construction grant for a septic system provided that no sewer service was given to any parcel of land within any wetlands, which included the owners' lots under the 1992 Policy. On appeal, the court found that the owners' lots were not similarly situated to non-mapped wetland lots that received sewer connections. The distinction between mapped and non-mapped wetlands under the 1992 Policy bore a rational relationship to legitimate state interests of fairness, fiscal integrity, and protection of ecological areas. Thus, the owners' equal protection rights under U.S. Const. amend. XIV and Md. Const. Decl. Rights art. 24 were not violated. There was no taking under U.S. Const. amend. V and Md. Const. art. III, § 40 because the owners failed to demonstrate that the denial of their permits under the 1992 Policy was a proximate cause of their lots being undevelopable and the denials fell within the takings nuisance exception, as leaking septic tanks contaminated the water.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment.

Myers v. State


No. 132, September Term, 2005


COURT OF APPEALS OF MARYLAND


395 Md. 261; 909 A.2d 1048; 2006 Md. LEXIS 707



October 24, 2006, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Certiorari to the Court of Special Appeals (Circuit Court for Washington County). Case No. 21-K-04-034537. Frederick C. Wright, III, JUDGE.
Myers v. State, 165 Md. App. 502, 885 A.2d 920, 2005 Md. App. LEXIS 280 (2005)



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT OF THE COURT OF SPECIAL APPEALS IS AFFIRMED. PETITIONER TO PAY THE COSTS IN THIS COURT AND IN THE COURT OF SPECIAL APPEALS.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The court granted certiorari to petitioner to challenge the judgment of the Court of Special Appeals (Maryland), which affirmed petitioner's conviction of theft of property having a value of $ 500 or greater and his sentence of 10 years imprisonment. The trial court had denied petitioner's motion to suppress and admitted into evidence several stolen items which were recovered from a residence.


OVERVIEW: On his prior appeal, petitioner challenged the trial court's denial of his motion to suppress evidence, the alleged illegality of his arrest, and the legal sufficiency of the evidence. Petitioner had been stopped in the State of Pennsylvania on suspicion of a traffic violation and of involvement in various burglaries there. After the stop, it was discovered that an outstanding arrest warrant for him existed. During the search incident to the arrest, the evidence of the stolen items from both the Maryland and Pennsylvania burglaries was discovered. Subsequently, a Pennsylvania court determined that the initial stop was illegal and violated petitioner's Fourth Amendment rights. Petitioner based his motion to suppress on the illegality of the Pennsylvania stop. The court upheld the denial of petitioner's motion to suppress upon finding that petitioner's arrest pursuant to an outstanding arrest warrant sufficiently attenuated the taint of the traffic stop in Pennsylvania. The court held that any taint from the unconstitutional seizure was dissipated by the subsequent discovery of the outstanding warrant, the subsequent legal arrest on that warrant, and the search incident thereto.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the lower court.

 

194 of 744 DOCUMENTS


Md. Overpak Corp. v. Mayor of Baltimore


No. 76, September Term, 2005


COURT OF APPEALS OF MARYLAND


395 Md. 16; 909 A.2d 235; 2006 Md. LEXIS 693



October 16, 2006, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Circuit Court for Baltimore City pursuant to certiorari to the Court of Special Appeals. Case # 24-C-04-009648. Michel W. Pierson, JUDGE.
Md. Overpak v. Baltimore, 389 Md. 398, 885 A.2d 823, 2005 Md. LEXIS 672 (2005)



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT OF THE CIRCUIT COURT FOR BALTIMORE CITY REVERSED; CASE REMANDED TO THE CIRCUIT COURT FOR PROCEEDINGS CONSISTENT WITH THIS OPINION; COSTS TO BE PAID BY APPELLEE.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant landowner sought judicial review in the Circuit Court for Baltimore City (Maryland) of appellee mayor and city council's approval of an amendment to a developer's planned unit development proposal. The trial court dismissed the petition, and the owner appealed to the Maryland Court of Special Appeals. The Maryland Court of Appeals issued a writ of certiorari before the intermediate appellate court considered the matter.


OVERVIEW: The trial court held it had no jurisdiction. The court of appeals held the amendment was a "zoning action" under Md. Ann. Code art. 66B, § 2.09(a)(1)(ii), giving the trial court jurisdiction. The city council's decision was made on grounds focused on a development plan for that plot of land only, rather than an entire zoning district, and thus was considered on an individualized basis. This individual action was the kind of change, focusing on the particulars involved with a specific property and its unique circumstances, contemplated as quasi-judicial in nature. The ordinance approving the amendment was enacted as the result of a quasi-judicial process approving an increase of the number of residential dwelling units permitted and modifying the uses and buildings permitted and their locations and size. The prevailing purpose of the ordinance was to define permissible uses of the specific parcel. The city council's deliberative consideration, after receiving evidence at a required hearing, and approval of specific uses for a specific parcel after making required statutory findings was within the realm of a "zoning action." Therefore, the landowner was entitled to judicial review.


OUTCOME: The trial court's judgment was reversed, and the matter was remanded.

Ehrlich v. Perez


No. 137, September Term, 2005


COURT OF APPEALS OF MARYLAND


394 Md. 691; 908 A.2d 1220; 2006 Md. LEXIS 691



October 12, 2006, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Circuit Court for Montgomery County pursuant to certiorari to the Court of Special Appeals. Durke G. Thompson, JUDGE.
Ehrlich v. Perez, 2006 Md. LEXIS 964 (Md., Mar. 9, 2006)



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT OF THE CIRCUIT COURT FOR MONTGOMERY COUNTY AFFIRMED IN PART AND VACATED IN PART; CASE REMANDED TO THAT COURT FOR FURTHER PROCEEDINGS. COSTS TO BE PAID BY APPELLANTS.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, the Governor of Maryland, the State Treasurer, and the Secretary of State Department of Health and Mental Hygiene, challenged the order of the Circuit Court for Montgomery County (Maryland), which granted a preliminary injunction in favor of appellees, minor resident aliens, by their next friends, and pregnant woman denied medical benefits under Md. Code Ann., Health-Gen. § 15-301(a)(2)(viii) (1982, Repl. Vol. 2005).


OVERVIEW: Appellants did not appropriate monies for the State Medical Assistance Program to resident alien children and pregnant women who immigrated to the United States on or after August 22, 1996, for Fiscal Year 2006 when it funded the same benefits to citizens and resident aliens who arrived before that date. Federal law expressly provided states with complete discretion to provide wholly state-funded medical benefits to legal residents. The trial court found that the failure of appellants to appropriate funds for medical benefits under § 15-301(a)(2)(viii) violated Md. Const. Decl. Rights art. 24 and ordered reimbursement of the benefits from the date of the original complaint until final disposition of the case. The court agreed that the failure to appropriate monies for the benefits on the basis of alienage violated art. 24, because the State's reasons for the alienage classification, namely cost savings, was not tailored suitably to serve a compelling state interest. However, the award of retrospective benefits was inappropriate in as much as it was, in effect, an award of past damages to appellees without any determination of appellees' actual damages suffered, if any.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed, in part, that portion of the order that affirmatively and prospectively enjoined appellants to provide benefits but vacated, in part, that portion of the order for relief that retrospectively granted benefits to appellees since such action was an award of past damages to appellees without either a final disposition on the merits or a determination of actual damages, if any, suffered by appellees.

Schisler v. State


No. 140, September Term, 2005


COURT OF APPEALS OF MARYLAND


394 Md. 519; 907 A.2d 175; 2006 Md. LEXIS 618



September 14, 2006, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal after remand at Schisler v. State, 2007 Md. App. LEXIS 160 (Md. Ct. Spec. App., Dec. 31, 2007)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Circuit Court for Baltimore City. Case # 24-C-06-005386. Albert J. Matricciani, Jr., JUDGE.
Schisler v. State, 2006 Md. LEXIS 454 (Md., July 7, 2006)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, individually, as Chairman of the Public Service Commission (PSC) and purportedly on behalf of those members of the PSC similarly situated (Commissioners), challenged the Circuit Court for Baltimore City's (Maryland) denial of a motion for temporary restraining order and preliminary injunction. The Chairman sought to stay §§ 12 and 22 of Senate Bill 1 (Md.), which terminated the Commissioners' appointments, from taking effect.


OVERVIEW: The General Assembly convened in a special session to consider and pass Senate Bill 1 (Md.). The governor vetoed the bill, and the Senate overrode the governor's veto. The bill was enacted as an "emergency bill" and went into effect immediately. The bill made various changes to the Public Utilities Article in response to an anticipated 72 percent increase in the cost of electricity. The Commissioners were appointed by the governor and served as civil officers of the state. The Chairman contended that §§ 12 and 22 violated various constitutional provisions. The appellate court held that the attempt by the General Assembly to remove the incumbent members of the Commission was null and void as an unconstitutional usurpation of the removal power granted to the governor under the provisions of Article II, § 15, and was further null and void as being in violation of Article II, §§ 1 and 9 and as being contrary to the provisions of Md. Const. Decl. Rights art. 8. It necessarily follows that the provision in § 22 of Senate Bill 1 (Md.) that attempted to delegate legislative power to the Attorney General to terminate the members of the Commission was also unconstitutional, null, and void.


OUTCOME: The appellate court reversed the trial court's decision and remanded to the trial court with instructions to render a declaratory judgment and permanent injunction consistent with the opinion.

Fowler v. Motor Vehicle Admin.


No. 111, September Term, 2005


COURT OF APPEALS OF MARYLAND


394 Md. 331; 906 A.2d 347; 2006 Md. LEXIS 544



August 30, 2006, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Certiorari to the Circuit Court for Montgomery County. Cas # 256013. S. Michael Pincus, JUDGE.
Fowler v. MVA, 390 Md. 500, 889 A.2d 418, 2006 Md. LEXIS 28 (2006)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner driver requested a hearing before respondent Motor Vehicle Administration (MVA) to contest a license suspension. The driver filed a motion requesting a subpoena for the arresting officer. The administrative law judge (ALJ) denied the driver's subpoena request and suspended the driver's license. The Circuit Court for Montgomery County (Maryland) affirmed. The driver appealed.


OVERVIEW: The issue before the appeals court was whether, during a driver license suspension hearing conducted under Md. Code Ann., Transp. § 16-205.1 (2002), an ALJ, faced with conflicting evidence in the form of an arresting police officer's written certification in a DR-15 Advice of Rights form and testimony from the arrested driver, may deny the driver's motion to subpoena the officer where the driver disputed that the driver was fully advised by the officer of the consequence for refusing to take a chemical breath test. The appeals court found that the administrative record provided no specific or explicit statement indicating whether the ALJ accepted or rejected the driver's proffered testimony. Thus, the appeals court was unable to perform its function. The appeals court also noted that the denial of the subpoena request was inappropriate given the factual circumstances of the case, noting that there was a factual dispute regarding whether the driver was fully advised of the consequences of refusing the breath test, as required by § 16-205.1, or whether the driver was misled by the officer's statements.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the circuit court was denied and the case was remanded with directions to reverse the decision of the MVA and remand to the office of administrative hearings for further proceedings.

 

198 of 744 DOCUMENTS


Duckworth v. Deane


No. 101, September Term, 2004


COURT OF APPEALS OF MARYLAND


393 Md. 524; 903 A.2d 883; 2006 Md. LEXIS 463



July 28, 2006, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Corrected August 3, 2006.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Circuit Court for Baltimore City pursuant to certiorari to the Court of Special Appeals. M. Brooke Murdock, JUDGE.
Duckworth v. Deane, 385 Md. 509, 869 A.2d 862, 2005 Md. LEXIS 109 (2005)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, a court clerk, eight legislators, and a private citizen, moved the Circuit Court for Baltimore City (Maryland) to intervene in an action challenging the constitutionality of Md. Code Ann., Fam. Law § 2-201 (2004). The trial court denied the motions to intervene, appellants sought review, and, prior to argument in the Maryland Court of Special Appeals, the Maryland Court of Appeals issued a writ of certiorari.


OVERVIEW: The underlying case contested the constitutionality of Md. Code Ann., Fam. Law § 2-201 (2004), defining a valid marriage. The court of appeals held the clerk could not intervene by private counsel. No exceptions to Md. Code Ann., State Gov't § 6-106(b) and (c), requiring his representation by the Attorney General, applied. No Maryland statute gave any appellant a right to intervene, under Md. R. 2-214(a)(1). The citizen's and legislators' interest did not differ from the public. The general assembly might have a different institutional "interest," but individual members had no greater legal interest than other citizens. The Declaratory Judgment Act, Md. Code Ann., Cts. & Jud. Proc. § 3-405(a), did not let the legislators intervene as (1) they had no interest affected by a declaration, under Md. Code Ann., Cts. & Jud. Proc. § 3-405(a)(1), and (2) if they met Md. R. 2-214(a)(2)'s "interest" requirement, no appellants showed existing parties might not represent their interests. A claim that the trial court could not find the statute's constitutionality was frivolous, so the Attorney General's refusal to argue it was no basis to intervene.


OUTCOME: The trial court's judgment denying appellants' motions to intervene was affirmed.

Trembow v. Schonfeld


No. 64, September Term, 2005


COURT OF APPEALS OF MARYLAND


393 Md. 327; 901 A.2d 825; 2006 Md. LEXIS 343



June 8, 2006, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal fron the Circuit Court for Frederick County pursuant to certiorari to the Court of Special Appeals. Case No. C-03-2097. Mary Ann Stepler, JUDGE.
Trembow v. Schonfeld, 388 Md. 673, 882 A.2d 286, 2005 Md. LEXIS 554 (Md., 2005)



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT OF CIRCUIT COURT FOR FREDERICK COUNTY AFFIRMED, WITH COSTS.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant mother challenged the judgment of the Circuit Court for Frederick County (Maryland), which dismissed the mother's complaint for child support upon the motion of appellee, an alleged father. The mother had filed the child support action against the alleged father claiming that the parties' son was a destitute adult in need of support.


OVERVIEW: The parties were never married. The child was born in March 1983. The mother claimed that the child was diagnosed with a genetic degenerative bone disorder at the age of 13, as a result of which he had become permanently disabled before reaching the age of 18. The complaint alleged that the child resided with the mother and, by reason of his physical disability, was unable to earn sufficient means to provide for himself. Previously, the mother never sought to have the alleged father's paternity established nor did she collect child support from him. Upon review of the legislative intent behind various statutes and the laws of other states, the court held that the mother was not entitled to pursue a paternity action. The court noted that, had the mother established paternity prior to the child's 18th birthday, she would have been entitled to seek child support for the destitute adult child and the child, directly or, if incompetent, through a guardian, was independently entitled to seek to establish paternity prior to reaching that point and, upon the ascertainment of paternity, to recover child support both during minority and, as a destitute adult child, thereafter.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment.


 

200 of 744 DOCUMENTS


Giddins v. State


No. 84, September Term, 2005


COURT OF APPEALS OF MARYLAND


393 Md. 1; 899 A.2d 139; 2006 Md. LEXIS 259



May 12, 2006, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY: Certiorari to the Court of Special Appeals (Circuit Court for Worcester County). Theodore R. Eschenburg, JUDGE.
Giddins v. State, 163 Md. App. 322, 878 A.2d 687, 2005 Md. App. LEXIS 98 (2005)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of the judgment of the Court of Special Appeals of Maryland affirming the denial of his motion to bar retrial following mistrial after the trial court had granted defendant's motion for mistrial during direct examination of the State's first witness.


OVERVIEW: During the State's first witness, the trial court declared a mistrial because the State elicited testimony from the witness regarding the search warrant that was inadmissible at trial and that had been previously precluded by the trial judge. After the jury had been released, the trial judge stated that, in his opinion, the State had engaged in prosecutorial misconduct and could not retry defendant. Defendant argued that the declaration of a mistrial constituted an acquittal that barred defendant's retrial under the Double Jeopardy Clause of the Fifth Amendment. If the trial court, in declaring a mistrial, ruled on the sufficiency or weight of the State's evidence, this constituted an acquittal for double jeopardy purposes. The trial judge granted a mistrial because the State elicited inadmissible evidence from a witness. The trial judge did not rule on the sufficiency or weight of the evidence. Furthermore, the trial judge had no evidence before it on which it could have ruled, since the witness had only testified as to the his name and employment.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the intermediate appellate court was affirmed.
